<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41597"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of Kobresia littledalei , the first chromosome-level genome in the family Cyperaceae"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-11"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Kobresia plants are important forage resources in the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau and are essential in maintaining the ecological balance of grasslands. Therefore, it is beneficial to obtain Kobresia genome resources and study the adaptive characteristics of Kobresia plants in the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. We assembled the genome of Kobresia littledalei C. B. Clarke, which was about 373.85&#8201;Mb in size. 96.82% of the bases were attached to 29 pseudo-chromosomes, combining PacBio, Illumina and Hi-C sequencing data. Additional investigation of the annotation identified 23,136 protein-coding genes. 98.95% of these were functionally annotated. According to phylogenetic analysis, K. littledalei in Cyperaceae separated from Poaceae about 97.6 million years ago after separating from Ananas comosus in Bromeliaceae about 114.3mya. For K. littledalei, we identified a high-quality genome at the chromosome level. This is the first time a reference genome has been established for a species of Cyperaceae. This genome will help additional studies focusing on the processes of plant adaptation to environments with high altitude and cold weather. 
                
                  
                    
                    
                    
                  
                
               Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: 
                https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12326612
                
              "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Kobresia</i> plants are important forage resources in the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau and are essential in maintaining the ecological balance of grasslands. Therefore, it is beneficial to obtain <i>Kobresia</i> genome resources and study the adaptive characteristics of <i>Kobresia</i> plants in the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. We assembled the genome of <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> C. B. Clarke, which was about 373.85âMb in size. 96.82% of the bases were attached to 29 pseudo-chromosomes, combining PacBio, Illumina and Hi-C sequencing data. Additional investigation of the annotation identified 23,136 protein-coding genes. 98.95% of these were functionally annotated. According to phylogenetic analysis<i>, K. littledalei</i> in Cyperaceae separated from Poaceae about 97.6 million years ago after separating from <i>Ananas comosus</i> in Bromeliaceae about 114.3mya. For <i>K. littledalei</i>, we identified a high-quality genome at the chromosome level. This is the first time a reference genome has been established for a species of Cyperaceae. This genome will help additional studies focusing on the processes of plant adaptation to environments with high altitude and cold weather.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Measurement(s)
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          genome â¢ DNA â¢ sequence_assembly â¢ sequence feature annotation
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                          sequencing platform
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Qinghai-Tibet Plateau, known as the âroof of the worldâ, is a vast alpine steppe with harsh natural conditions of high altitude, cold, intense ultraviolet radiation and drought. After a long period of natural selection, most of the forage germplasm resources in this area have desirable genes such as resistance to cold and drought, which are indispensable materials for breeding improved varieties of crop plants. Tibetâs natural grasslands are rich in wild forage germplasm resources, and <i>Kobresia</i> plants (Cyperaceae) are the most important component of these alpine grasslands. <i>Kobresia</i> plants are perennial herbs that are mainly distributed in temperate to cold zones of the Northern Hemisphere and are mostly concentrated in the Himalayas and Hengduan Mountains. <i>Kobresia</i> plants are important forage resources in the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau due to their nutritious features. In addition, <i>Kobresia</i> plants are essential in maintaining the ecological balance of grasslands because they are tolerant of cold, radiation, drought and strong wind. <i>K. littledalei</i> are mainly distributed in low-lying areas along the edge of lakes and rivers and are used as mowed grasslands and winter grazing grasslands (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="609"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>A representative individual of <i>Kobresia littledalei</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>K. littledalei</i> genome was assembled and annotated using long reads obtained from the PacBio Sequel sequencing program and short reads from the Illumina Hi-seq. 2500 sequencing program. We determined that the final genome assembly has a contig N50 of ~2.55âMb and is approximately 373.85âMb. Using Hi-C data, we determined that 96.28% of the assembled bases were associated with 29 pseudo-chromosomes. <i>K. littledalei</i> represents the first assembled genome in Cyperaceae. We identified 23,136 protein-coding genes from the generated assembly, annotating 98.95% (22,892 genes) of all the protein-coding genes. We determined that <i>K. littledalei</i> separated from Poaceae about 97.6 million years ago after separating from Bromeliaceae about 114.3 million years ago. The genome assembly of <i>K. littledalei</i> provides an important framework for the additional study of adaption to environment of high altitude and cold weather and promote the protection of the environment in the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau.</p><p>In Poales, genomes of some species in Poaceae and one species in Bromeliaceae have been sequenced and assembled. Cyperaceae is more closely related to Poaceae than Bromeliaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="MagallÃ³n, S., GÃ³mez-Acevedo, S., SÃ¡nchez-Reyes, L. L. &amp; HernÃ¡ndez-HernÃ¡ndez, T. A metacalibrated time-tree documents the early rise of flowering plant phylogenetic diversity. New Phytol. 207, 437â453 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d35058e650">1</a></sup>, and the assembly of the genome <i>K. littledalei</i> offers an opportunity for the investigation of Poales evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sample sequencing and genome size estimation</h3><p>High-quality genomic DNA for sequencing was extracted from leaf tissue of <i>K. littledalei</i>, which was collected in July 2018 from DangXiong in the Tibet Autonomous Region of China. The sample was in anthesis, and located at altitudes of up to 4,263âm.</p><p>The Illumina library with insert sizes of 350âbp was arranged with a Genomic DNA Sample Preparation kit from Illumina. It was then sequenced using a HiSeq 2500 platform, also from Illumina. This yielded 168.79 million reads, <span class="stix">â¼</span>50.64âGb of raw sequence data, which covered ~121.95X of the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s1</a>). Large DNA fragments longer than 10âkb were enriched and were then sequenced using a PacBio Sequel system. From this, we obtained 5,618,892 reads that had an N50 length of 17,273 base pairs and a mean of 11,099 base pairs. In total, 62.37G bases were obtained, which is ~150.20X coverage of the genome (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s1</a>). Leaf tissue of <i>K. littledalei</i> was used to construct a library for Hi-C analysis, and the NEBNext Ultra II DNA library Prep Kit from Illumina (NEB) was used to prepare the Hi-C library, which we then sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform. 230,316,080 paired-end reads of 150âbp were obtained from the Illumina sequencing platform for the Hi-C library, which covered ~166.40X of the genome (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s1</a>).</p><p>The size and heterozygosity level of the <i>K. littledalei</i> genome were estimated through k-mer spectrum analysis using sequences generated by Illumina DNA sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Xiao, Y., Xiao, Z., Ma, D., Liu, J. &amp; Li, J. Genome sequence of the barred knifejaw Oplegnathus fasciatus (Temminck &amp; Schlegel, 1844): the first chromosome-level draft genome in the family Oplegnathidae. GigaScience. 8, 21â22 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d35058e693">2</a></sup>. The depth distribution of the derived 17-mers clearly showed two separate peaks and the main volume peak of k-mer frequency was 96, based on which we estimated the heterozygosity level and repeat frequencies of the <i>K. littledalei</i> genome to be 1.68% and 53.93%, respectively; the genome size was estimated to be 415.24âMb (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s1</a>, Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Assembly of the <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> C. B. Clarke genome</h3><p>First, PacBio long reads were self-corrected to obtain pre-assembly reads. The pre-assembly reads were assembled into consensus sequences by FALCON through the âOverlap-Layout-Consensusâ algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods. 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35058e718">3</a></sup>. Consensus sequences were corrected using Illumina short reads to improve the precision in Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d35058e722">4</a></sup>. The preliminary genome assembly of <i>K. littledalei</i> includes 1210 contigs with N50â=â2,253,412âbp and longest scaffoldâ=â11,050,451âbp. The genome is approximately 759âM in length and the GC content of the genome is 35.74% (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s3</a>).</p><p>Purge Haplotigs was used to filter redundant sequences due to heterozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Roach, M. J., Schmidt, S. &amp; Borneman, A. R. Purge Haplotigs: synteny reduction for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinformatics. 19, 460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35058e735">5</a></sup>. The final assembled <i>K. littledalei</i> genome contained 212 scaffolds with an N50 length of 3,054,069âbp and a cumulative size of 373,821,983âbp. The longest scaffold reached 11,045,779âbp, and the GC content of the genome was 35.44% (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Tab1">1</a>)</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of assembled <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> C. B. Clarke assembly and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We used the procedures described by DC. Zhang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zhang, D.-C. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly of golden pompano (Trachinotus ovatus) in the family Carangidae. Scientific Data. 6, 216 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1059">6</a></sup> to anchor the scaffolds into pseudo-chromosomes. We first used HiCUP v0.6.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wingett, S. et al. HiCUP: pipeline for mapping and processing Hi-C data. F1000Research. 4, 35â36 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1063">7</a></sup> to map and process reads obtained from the Hi-C library. Each of the reads from one pair were uniquely mapped to the assembly and kept for downstream filtration. Invalid pairs generated from fragments of the wrong size, PCR duplication, re-ligation, internal fragments, dangling ends, circularization, and contiguous sequences were removed. <i>K. littledalei</i> has 2nâ=â58 chromosomes, as determined by karyotype analyses. We corrected some small errors in the results of the FALCON assembly by clustering contigs with the contig contact frequency matrix. We obtained 523 total contigs by grouping contigs with errors into shorter contigs. Using Lachesis v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1070">8</a></sup> (pseudo-chromosome number set as 29), we clustered 337 contigs into pseudo-chromosomes using the refined alignments. This corresponds to 96.28% of the assembly by base count and 64.44% of the assembly by sequence number.</p><p>We built an interaction matrix by HiC-Pro using clean reads from the Hi-C library to confirm the accuracy of the Hi-C scaffolding at the pseudo-chromosome level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1077">9</a></sup>. The genome was split into equal-sized bins of 500k, while the contact numbers were designated between each pair of bins. We confirmed the genome quality and structure using a contact map plotted with HiCPlotter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Akdemir, K. C. &amp; Chin, L. HiCPlotter integrates genomic data with interaction matrices. Genome Biol. 16, 198 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1081">10</a></sup> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="591"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Heat map of chromatin contact matrices generated by aligning a Hi-C dataset to the <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> genome. The frequency of interactions was calculated using a window size of 500âK.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Repeat annotation</h3><p>We used RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. 25, 4.10.11â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1117">11</a></sup> to predict repeat sequences of the <i>K. littledalei</i> genome through homology searching of repetitive elements released by Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mobile DNA. 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1124">12</a></sup> and <i>ab initio</i> identified by LTR Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1131">13</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 21, 351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1136">14</a></sup> and RepeatModeler. We identified a total of ~202.34âM repetitive elements, which was 54.13% of the genome, after integrating ~40.04âM repetitive elements predicted by RepeatProteinMask and ~20.96âM tandem repetitive sequences predicted by TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1140">15</a></sup> (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s4</a>). Among them, DNA transposons accounted for 18.53% of the genome, while long terminal repeat (LTR), long interspersed nuclear elements (LINE) and short interspersed nuclear elements (SINE) belonging to retrotransposons accounted for 27.87%, 5.42% and 0.10% of the genome respectively (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Prediction and functional annotation of protein-coding genes</h3><p>The repeat-masked <i>K. littledalei</i> C. B. Clarke genome was used for subsequent prediction of protein-coding genes, which integrates evidence from <i>de novo</i> predictions, protein homology and RNA transcripts. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Stanke, M., SchÃ¶ffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics. 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1164">16</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Pertea, M., Salzberg, S. L. &amp; Majoros, W. H. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1168">17</a></sup>, SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1172">18</a></sup>, Geneid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. Chapter 4, Unit 4.3 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1177">19</a></sup> and Genscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1181">20</a></sup> were used for <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction. For homolog searches, we used proteomes of <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Setaria italica</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Due to the lack of RNA-seq data of <i>K. littledalei</i>, we used the RNA-seq data from nine species from <i>Kobresia</i> species. In total, 26,046 primitive gene models were predicted after integrating results of the three sources of evidence by EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1213">21</a></sup>. We then filtered and polished these gene models through expression level and evidence number, and 22,979 genes with FPKMâ&gt;â1 or supported by more than two lines of evidence were retained (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s6</a>). For gene models only supported by one line of evidence, we searched SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The UniProt Consortium. UniProt: the universal protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 158â169 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1221">22</a></sup>, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Morishima, K., Tanabe, M., Furumichi, M., Kanehisa, M. &amp; Sato, Y. KEGG: new perspectives on genomes, pathways, diseases and drugs. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 353â361 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1225">23</a></sup>, NCBI-nr, InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bateman, A. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 211â215 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1229">24</a></sup> and Pfam for homologs. Gene models with homologs in any of the databases were retained, resulting in 157 genes. In total, 23,136 gene models were identified. The average length of genes and CDS are 3,545.25âbp and 1,163.41âbp, respectively, and there are 5.39 exons in each gene with length of 215.84âbp per exon (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Tab1">1</a>). Among them, 12,726 gene models are supported by all three lines of evidence (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s2</a>).</p><p>To assess the completeness of the gene identification, we conducted BUSCO analysis on 23,136 gene models. For 1,440 expected embryophyta genes, 86.2% complete and 3.6% fragmented gene models were identified in <i>K. littledalei</i>. The identified gene model was 89.8%, which is less than pearl millet (95.4%), broomcorn millet (98%), sugarcane (95.4%) and other recently published Poaceae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Pearl millet genome sequence provides a resource to improve agronomic traits in arid environments. Nat. Biotechnol. 35, 969â976 (2017)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1245">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zou, C. et al. The genome of broomcorn millet. Nature Commun. 10, 436 (2019)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1245_1">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, J. et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat. Genet. 50, 1565â1573 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1248">27</a></sup>. We also download the genome sequence of <i>A. comosus</i> and conducted BUSCO analysis; 92.6% complete and 2.7% fragmented gene models were identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1255">28</a></sup>. To explore the reason, we also conducted BUSCO analysis on the transcriptomes (leaf) of <i>Kobresia tibetica, K. royleana</i> and <i>K. pygmaea</i> assembled by Trinity, and 81.4%, 74.6% and 79.3% complete gene models plus 4.1%, 5.6% and 6.0% fragmented gene models were identified, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s7</a>). Simultaneously, the transcriptomes of three other Cyperaceae species, <i>Cyperus papyrus</i> (shoot without flower), <i>Lepidosperma gibsonii</i> (leaves and buds) and <i>Mapania palustris</i> (leaf shoots) were downloaded from the 1,000 plants (1KP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. GigaScience. 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1278">29</a></sup> project. The complete gene models were 57.1%, 64.7% and 39.5% and the fragmented gene models were 14.4%, 13.3% and 23.3% assessed by BUSCO analysis (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s7</a>).</p><p>Functional annotation of protein coding genes was obtained by mapping protein sequences to SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The UniProt Consortium. UniProt: the universal protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 158â169 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1289">22</a></sup>, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Morishima, K., Tanabe, M., Furumichi, M., Kanehisa, M. &amp; Sato, Y. KEGG: new perspectives on genomes, pathways, diseases and drugs. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 353â361 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1293">23</a></sup> and NCBI-nr protein databases by BLASTP to get the best hit. Simultaneously, functional annotation of protein coding genes was inferred by protein domains identified by searching the protein sequence against the InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bateman, A. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 211â215 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1297">24</a></sup> and Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bateman, A. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, 222â230 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1301">30</a></sup> databases using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Mitchell, A. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1305">31</a></sup> and HMMER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Johnson, L. S., Eddy, S. R. &amp; Portugaly, E. Hidden Markov model speed heuristic and iterative HMM search procedure. BMC Bioinformatics. 11, 431 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1310">32</a></sup>. The Gene Ontology (GO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Consortium, T. G. O. Gene Ontology Consortium: going forward. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, 1049â1056 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1314">33</a></sup> terms were obtained by Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Conesa, A. &amp; GÃ¶tz, S. Blast2GO: A comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics. 2008, 12 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1318">34</a></sup>. A total of 22,892 (98.95) out of 23,136 genes have integrated functional annotation (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome structure of <i>K. littledalei</i>
</h3><p>The genus <i>Kobresia</i>, which includes about 70 species, is distributed predominantly in the alpine mountains of the Northern Hemisphere, and a majority of the 59 species found throughout China live on the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau. The basic chromosome numbers of species in <i>Kobresia</i> vary a lot (xâ=â16, xâ=â26, xâ=â29 and so on), which indicates that great changes have occurred in the chromosome structure of <i>Kobresia</i>. Moreover, it is reported that more than one-half of the tested species are polyploid in <i>Kobresia</i>, which is high compared with 5.7% in the closely related genus <i>Carex</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lipnerova, I., Bures, P., Horova, L. &amp; Smarda, P. Evolution of genome size in Carex (Cyperaceae) in relation to chromosome number and genomic base composition. Ann. Bot-London. 111, 79â94 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1352">35</a></sup>. The changes of chromosome structure and polyploidization in Kobresia likely indicate how these species adapted to the harsh environment of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. The length of chromosomes of <i>K. littledalei</i> ranges from ~2.46âM to ~19.67âM. The centromeric regions were identified using an approach described by Robert <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature. 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1363">36</a></sup>. The base centromere repeat was 162âbp and highly abundant tandem repeats were identified on 18 chromosomes. The highly abundant tandem repeats dispersed on chromosome with high TE density like Chr1, Chr2, Chr5 and Chr16 (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Features of the <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Evolutionary and comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>To explore the evolutionary relationship of <i>K. littledalei</i>, we used OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Conesa, A. &amp; GÃ¶tz, S. Blast2GO: A comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics. 2008, 12 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1402">34</a></sup> to cluster its genes with those from eight other commelinid monocots: <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Z mays</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>Musa acuminata, Phyllostachys heterocycla</i> and one dicot <i>A. thaliana</i>. From these ten species, we identified 826 one-to-one single-copy genes that were used to construct a maximum likelihood (ML) tree to show the evolutionary relationships using RaxML with the GTRGAMMA model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics. 22, 2688â2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1431">37</a></sup>. Divergence times were estimated using the âmcmctreeâ program incorporated in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1436">38</a></sup>. According to the phylogenetic tree, <i>K. littledalei</i> separated from Poaceae about 97.6 million years ago after separating from Bromeliaceae about 114.3 million years ago (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig4">4a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="240"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The phylogenetic relationships and divergence times of commelinid plants, and contraction and expansion of gene families. (<b>a</b>) The phylogenetic relationships and divergence times of commelinid plants. Phylogenetic reconstructions using concatenation of 1,077 genes and the maximum likelihood (ML) method with <i>A. thaliana</i> as the distant outgroup. Divergence times were estimated using the âmcmctreeâ program incorporated in the PAML package. (<b>b</b>) Contraction and expansion of gene families. Numbers in green represent expanded families on this clade, and numbers in red represent contracted families on this clade.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To clarify the genome duplication history of <i>K. littledalei</i>, we screened the paralogs within syntenic blocks of <i>K. littledalei</i> by McScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science. 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1482">39</a></sup> and calculated the distribution of the rate of transversions on fourfold degenerate synonymous sites (4DTv). There is one peak with values of 4DTv at 0.63â0.68, which indicated that one whole genome duplication (WGD) event occurred before the rho WGD event that occurred ~70 MYA in the grass lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1486">40</a></sup>. To investigate the genome duplication history in Poales, we also screened the orthologs with syntenic blocks between <i>K. littledalei</i> and <i>A. comosus, O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> separately. Simultaneously, we calculated the 4DTv of the paralogs in <i>A. comosus, O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>, which showed an obvious WGD with a 4DTv value of 0.4. The 4DTv peaks between <i>K. littledalei</i> and <i>A. comosus, O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> are between 0.6â0.8, near the WGD of <i>K. littledalei</i> but earlier than the WGD of <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>. Taken together, Cyperaceae separated from Bromeliaceae and Poaceae during the time of the WGD of <i>K. littledalei</i> and they were subjected to WGD independently after differentiation (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0518-3/MediaObjects/41597_2020_518_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="337"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Whole genome duplication events in <i>Kobresia littledalei</i> and other Poaceae plants.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparing gene families among seven monocots, including <i>K. littledalei</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>E. guineensis</i>, <i>M. acuminata and P. heterocycla</i>, we identified 23,136â<i>K. littledalei</i> genes in 12,006 families, with 8,645 gene families shared among them and 117 gene families unique in <i>K. littledalei</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s3</a>). The expansion and contraction of the gene families, which can implicate the evolutionary dynamics of genes, were indicated by gene copy number in each family. In total, 17 gene families were contracted, and 55 gene families were expanded in <i>K. littledalei</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#Fig4">4b</a>). The expanded gene families included F-box containing protein, agamous-like MADS-box protein, and B3 domain containing protein (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s9</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The raw data of the whole genome was submitted to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) SRA with accession number SRP198441<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive&nbsp;
                  https://identifiers.org/insdc.sra:SRP198441
                  
                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1606">41</a></sup>. The final assembly and annotation had been deposited at GenBank SWLB00000000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Qu, G. Carex littledalei isolate C.B.Clarke, whole genome shotgun sequencing project. Genbank&nbsp;
                  https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:SWLB00000000
                  
                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1610">42</a></sup>. Gene functional annotations, repeat annotation and results of evolutionary analysis had been deposited at Figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Qu, G. Genome sequence of Kobresia littledalei, the first chromosome-level genome in the family Cyperaceae. figshare&nbsp;
                  https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12197544.v1
                  
                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1614">43</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Evaluation of the genome assembly</h3><p>For the final assembly, we used CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Parra, G., Korf, I. &amp; Bradnam, K. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1630">44</a></sup> to assess the completeness of the assembled <i>K. littledalei</i> genome, and 233 (93.95%) and nine (3,63%) genes out of 248 core eukaryotic genes had complete and partial alignment sequence in the assembly, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s10</a>). Of the 1,440 expected embryophyta genes, 84.5% were identified as having complete BUSCO profiles and 2.5% had fragmented BUSCO profiles of the 1440 expected embryophyta genes (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s11</a>). In total, 80.63% of the transcripts assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1643">45</a></sup> using <i>Kobresia</i> RNA-seq data covered 90% by one scaffold and 92.89% of transcripts covered 50% by one scaffold (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s12</a>). We evaluated the assembly continuity by analyzing the LTR Assembly Index (LAI)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res. 46, e126âe126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1654">46</a></sup>, which is a standard method of assessing repeat sequences. The <i>K. littledalei</i> LAI score is 14.8, which indicated good continuity of the assembly.</p><p>For the preliminarily assembled sequences, we also used BUSCO version 3 (BUSCO, embryophyta odb9) assessing the completeness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kriventseva, E. V., Zdobnov, E. M., SimÃ£o, F. A., Ioannidis, P. &amp; Waterhouse, R. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1664">47</a></sup>, and 87.5% of the 1440 expected embryophyta genes were identified as having complete BUSCO profiles (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s11</a>). This result indicated that some real genome sequences were deleted during fusing of haplotype contigs. We compared the missing BUSCO profiles among <i>K. littledalei</i> genome assembly, transcriptomes of <i>Kobresia tibetica</i>, <i>Kobresia royleana, Kobresia pygmaea, Cyperus papyrus, Lepidosperma gibsonii</i> and <i>Mapania palustris</i>, 84 out of 181 were common in Cyperaceae species (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s7</a>, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s4</a>). This indicated that these genes are missing in all Cyperaceae species or varies a lot in Cyperaceae species compared to other embryophyta.</p><p>We used SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience. 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1693">48</a></sup> to assemble the unmapped Illumina reads to final assembly (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s13</a>), and 48,147,213âbp of sequence were assembled with N50â=â282 and longest_contigâ=â7749 (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s14</a>). We identified 2,274 genes (Supplementary set in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s6</a>) from the 48.15âM SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience. 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d35058e1706">48</a></sup> assembled sequences. The average gene length and the number and length of exons were all less than genes identified from the final assembly, resulting from the short length of the assembly (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s6</a>). After combining these sequences with the final assembly, 0.6% of the fragmented expected embryophyta genes increased.</p><p>These results indicated that some portion of our genome assembly is still missing. And the heterozygosity of the genome was evaluated to 1.68% from survey analysis (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0518-3#MOESM1">s2</a>), which may increase the difficulty of the genome assembly.</p></div></div></section>
